    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_437320710" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/437320710" />
    <a title="Farhan Khalid" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2881911-farhan-khalid"><img alt="Farhan Khalid" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1350661583p2/2881911.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/437320710?book_show_action=true">Oct 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Farhan Khalid" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Farhan Khalid" href="/user/show/2881911-farhan-khalid">Farhan Khalid</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780871401625" href="/book/show/454750.The_Conquest_of_Happiness">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2881911-farhan-khalid?shelf=nobel">nobel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2881911-farhan-khalid?shelf=self-help">self-help</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2881911-farhan-khalid?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2881911-farhan-khalid?shelf=english">english</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer437320710" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9776512438547266504"><b>Unhappiness, why?</b><br/><br/>Day-to-day unhappiness from which most people in civilized countries suffer, and which is all the more unbearable because having no external cause, it appears inescapable<br/><br/>I believe this unhappiness to be very largely due to mistaken views of the world, mistaken ethics, mistaken habits of life<br/><br/><b>Extrospection</b><br/><br/>Gradually I learned to be indifferent to myself and my experience<br/><br/>I came to centre my attention increasingly upon external objects<br/><br/><b>Sins</b><br/><br/>Sins are committed by everyone or no one<br/><br/>The m</span>
  <span id="freeText9776512438547266504" style="display:none"><b>Unhappiness, why?</b><br /><br />Day-to-day unhappiness from which most people in civilized countries suffer, and which is all the more unbearable because having no external cause, it appears inescapable<br /><br />I believe this unhappiness to be very largely due to mistaken views of the world, mistaken ethics, mistaken habits of life<br /><br /><b>Extrospection</b><br /><br />Gradually I learned to be indifferent to myself and my experience<br /><br />I came to centre my attention increasingly upon external objects<br /><br /><b>Sins</b><br /><br />Sins are committed by everyone or no one<br /><br />The man who is absorbed in the consciousness of sin is perpetually incurring his own disapproval<br /><br />Liberation from the tyranny of early beliefs and affections is the first step towards happiness<br /><br /><b>Narcissism</b><br /><br />Habit of admiring oneself and wishing to be admired<br /><br />[in its excesses] when a woman of this kind is sure that a man loves her, she has no further use for him<br /><br />The man whose sole concern with the world is that it shall admire him is not likely to achieve his object<br /><br />But even if he does, he will not be completely happy<br /><br /><b>Megalomaniac</b><br /><br />The Megalomaniac differs from the narcissist by the fact that he wishes to be powerful rather than charming<br /><br />Napoleon suffered at school from inferiority to his schoolfellows<br /><br /><b>Distraction and oblivion</b><br /><br />A man may feel so completely thwarted that he seeks no form of satisfaction, but only distraction and oblivion<br /><br />He then becomes a devotee of "pleasure" <br /><br /><b>Absence of effort</b><br /><br />The mere absence of effort from man's life removes an essential ingredient of happiness<br /><br /><b>Transformation</b><br /><br />Many people have thrown over the old standards without acquiring the new ones. This leads them into various troubles as their unconscious usually still believe in the old standards<br /><br /><b>Love</b><br /><br />Love is to be valued because it enhances all the best pleasures<br /><br />Love is able to break down the hard shell of the ego<br /><br /><b>Tragedy</b><br /><br />To write tragedy, a man must feel tragedy. To feel tragedy, one must be aware of the world in which he lives<br /><br /><b>American enjoyment of existence</b><br /><br />"The struggle of life"<br /><br /><b>Poverty</b><br /><br />I have no doubt that those who have suffered greatly though poverty in their childhood are haunted by terrors lest their children should suffer similarly<br /><br /><b>Quieter pleasures</b><br /><br />All the quieter pleasures have been abandoned<br /><br />What use would such knowledge be? It could not add to anybody's income<br /><br /><b>Generally received philosophy</b><br /><br />Life is contest, a competition, in which respect is to be accorded to the victor<br /><br /><b>Competition</b><br /><br />Not only work is poisoned by the philosophy of competition, leisure is poisoned just as much<br /><br /><b>Boredom</b><br /><br />It is also one of the essentials of boredom that one's faculties must not be fully occupied<br /><br />Running away from enemies who are trying to take one's life is, I imagine, unpleasant, but not boring<br /><br />A man would not feel bored while he was being executed<br /><br /><b>Excitement</b><br /><br />The opposite of boredom is not pleasure but excitement<br /><br />A life too full of excitement is an exhausting life, in which continually stronger stimuli are needed to give the thrill that has come to be thought an essential part of pleasure <br /><br /><b>Persistent work</b><br /><br />All greatest lives have contained uninteresting stretches<br /><br />Kent is said never to have been more than ten miles from Konigsberg all his life<br /><br />Darwin, after going round the world spent the whole of the rest of his life in his own house<br /><br />Marx, after stirring up a few revolutions, decided to spend the remainder of his days in the British Museum<br /><br />Altogether it will be found that a quiet life is characteristic of great men, and that their pleasures have not been of the sort that would look exciting to the outward eye<br /><br />No great achievement is possible without persistent work<br /><br /><b>Fatigue</b> <br /><br />Fatigue is associated with strenuous work for a living<br /><br />Men take their business worries to bed with them<br /><br /><b>Anxiety</b><br /><br />A great many worries can be diminished by realizing the unimportance of the matter which is causing the anxiety<br /><br />The less I cared whether I spoke well of badly, the less badly I spoke<br /><br /><b>Unconscious</b><br /><br />Most of the unconscious consists of what were once highly emotional conscious thoughts, which have now become buried<br /><br />"Well, after all, that would not matter so very much"<br /><br />"Well, after all, that would not matter so very much"<br /><br />"Well, after all, that would not matter so very much"<br /><br />You will find that your worry diminishes to a quite extraordinary extent and is replaced by a kind of exhilaration <br /><br />Worry is a form of fear and all forms of fear produce fatigue<br /><br />Every kind of fear grows worse by not being looked at<br /><br /><b>Courage</b><br /><br />Given more courage there would be less worry, and therefore less fatigue<br /><br />A very frequent source of fatigue is love of excitement<br /><br /><b>Envy</b><br /><br />Envy is the basis of democracy<br /><br />Envy is seeing things never in themselves but only in their relations<br /><br />One begins to be eaten up with a sense of injustice<br /><br />If you desire glory, you may envy Napoleon. But Napoleon envied Caesar, Caesar envied Alexander, and Alexander, I daresay, envied Hercules, who never existed<br /><br />I do not believe that any peacock envies another peacock's tail, because every peacock is persuaded that his own tail is the finest in the world. The consequence of this is that peacock are peaceable birds<br /><br />Envy, is of course, closely connected with competition<br /><br />In an age when the social hierarchy is fixed, the lowest classes do not envy the upper classes so long as the division between rich and poor is thought to be ordained by God<br /><br />Beggar do not envy millionaires, though of course will envy other beggars who are more successful<br /><br /><b>Hatred</b><br /><br />The human heart as modern civilization has made it is more prone to hatred than to friendship <br /><br />And it is prone to hatred because it is dissatisfied <br /><br /><b>Sense of sin</b><br /><br />The sense of sin in its most important forms is something which goes deeper<br /><br />It is something which has roots in the unconscious, and does not appear in the consciousness as fear of other people's approval<br /><br />In consciousness certain kinds of acts are labelled as Sin for no reason visible to introspection <br /><br />When a man commits these acts he feels uncomfortable without quite knowing why<br /><br /><b>Divided man</b><br /><br />The man divided against himself looks for excitement and distraction, he loves strong passions, not for sound reasons, but because for the moment they take him outside himself and prevent the painful necessity of thought<br /><br />The happiness that is genuinely satisfying is accompanied by the fullest exercise of our faculties, and the fullest realization of the world in which we live<br /><br /><b>Love of power</b><br /><br />Love of power is insidious. It has many disguises, and is often the source of the pleasure we derive from doing what we believe to be good to other people<br /><br /><b>Fear of public persecution</b><br /><br />To almost everybody sympathetic surroundings are necessary to happiness<br /><br />If it is to become possible, some way must be found by which the tyranny of public opinion can be either lessened or evaded, and by which members of the intelligent minority can come to know each other and enjoy each other's society<br /><br />Fear of this kind remains strong, it is impossible to acquire that freedom of spirit which in which true happiness consists<br /><br />It is essential to happiness that our way of living should spring from our own deep impulses and not from the accidental tastes and desires of those who happen to be our neighbors, or even our relations<br /> <br />There is new kind of fear, namely the fear of what newspaper may say<br /><br />I have been almost led to conclude that happiness in the modern world has become an impossibility. This view tends to be dissipated by introspection, foreign travel, and the conversation of my gardener<br /><br /><b>Two sorts of happiness</b><br /><br />Happiness is of two sorts, of course, there are intermediate degrees<br /><br />The two sorts might be distinguished as plain and fancy, or animal and spiritual, or of the heart and of the head<br /><br />Pleasures of achievement demand difficulties such that beforehand success seems doubtful although in the end it is usually achieved<br /><br /><b>Men of science</b><br /><br />Domestic bliss in the lives of men of science<br /><br />The reason this is that the higher parts of their intelligence are wholly absorbed by their work<br /> <br />They have therefore no necessity for complex emotions, since the simpler emotions meet with no obstacles<br /><br /><b>Complex emotions</b><br /><br />Complexity in emotions is like foam in a river. It is produced by obstacles which break the smoothly flowing current<br /><br />Companionship and co-operation are essential elements in the happiness of the average man<br /><br />Belief in a cause is a source of happiness to large numbers of people<br /><br /><b>I collect rivers</b><br /><br />For my part, I collect rivers<br /><br />I derive pleasure from having gone down the Volga and up the Yangtse, and regret very much having never seen the Amazon or the Orinoco<br /><br /><b>Hobbies and interests</b><br /><br />Fads and hobbies, however, are in many cases, perhaps, most, not a source of fundamental happiness, but a means of escape from reality, of forgetting for the moment some pain too difficult to be faces<br /><br />A friendly interest in people is a form of affectionateness, but not the form which is grasping and possessiveness and seeking always an emphatic response<br /><br /><b>The secret of happiness</b><br /><br />Let your interests be as wide as possible, and let your reactions to the things and person interest be as far as possible friendly rather than hostile<br /><br />All the separate tastes and desires have to fit into the general framework of life. If they are to be a source of happiness they must be compatible with health. With the affection of those we love, and the respect of the society in which we live<br /><br /><b>Zest</b><br /><br />Zest demands energy more that sufficient for the necessary work<br /><br />The feeling of being loved promotes zest more than anything else does<br /><br /><b>Unloved man</b><br /><br />Man, observing that he is unloved, may seek revenge upon the world, either by stirring up wars and revolutions, or by a pen dipped in gall, like Dean Swift<br /><br />For those who make themselves the slaves of unvarying routine are generally actuated by fear of a cold outer world<br /><br />Those who face life with a feeling of security are much happier than those who face it with a feeling of insecurity<br /><br /><b>Difficult past</b><br /><br />The child from whom for any reason parental affection is withdrawn is likely to become timid and unadventurous, filled with fears and self-pity, and no longer able to meet the world in a mood of gay exploration<br /><br />Such a child may set to work at a surprisingly early age to meditate on life and death and human destiny<br /><br />He becomes an introvert, melancholy at first, but seeking ultimately the unreal consolations of system of philosophy and theology<br /><br /><b>Affection</b><br /><br />If he had received more affection, would have feared the real world less, and would not have had to invent an ideal world to take its place in his beliefs<br /><br />Affection in the sense of a genuine reciprocal interest of two persons in each other, not solely as means to each other's good, but rather as a combination having a common good, is one of the most important elements of real happiness<br /><br /><b>Powerful ego</b><br /><br />A too powerful ego is a prison from which a man must escape if he is to enjoy the world to the full<br /><br /><b>Parenthood</b> <br /><br />If the white race are to survive, that parenthood should again become capable of yielding happiness to parents<br /><br />Parenthood is psychologically capable of providing the greatest and most enduring happiness that life has to offer<br /><br /><b>Work</b><br /><br />All skilled work can be pleasurable<br /><br />One of the causes of unhappiness among intellectuals in the present day is that so many of them, especially those whose skills is literary, find no opportunity for the independent exercise of their talents<br /><br />The man who is ashamed of his work can hardly achieve self-respect<br /><br />The habit of viewing life as a whole is an essential part both of wisdom and of true morality<br /><br />Consistent purpose embodies itself mainly in work<br /><br /><b>Impersonal interests</b><br /><br />For a man of a bookish turn of mind reading unconnected with his professional activities is very satisfactory<br /><br />Impersonal interests help a man to retain his sense of proportion <br /><br />How small a part this is of the total of human activity and how many things in the world are entirely unaffected by what we do<br /><br /><b>Defects of higher education</b><br /><br />It is one of the defects of modern higher education that it has become too much a training in the acquisition of certain kinds of skill, and too little an enlargement of the mind and heart<br /><br /><b>The happy man</b><br /><br />The happy man is the man who lives objectively, who has free affections and wide interest, who secures his happiness through these interest and affections and through the fact that they, in turn, make him an object of interest and affection</span>
  <a data-text-id="9776512438547266504" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_62231154" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/62231154" />
    <a title="P.J. Sullivan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2259722-p-j-sullivan"><img alt="P.J. Sullivan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442474532p2/2259722.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/62231154?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="P.J. Sullivan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="P.J. Sullivan" href="/user/show/2259722-p-j-sullivan">P.J. Sullivan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2259722-p-j-sullivan?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2259722-p-j-sullivan?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2259722-p-j-sullivan?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2259722-p-j-sullivan?shelf=social-psychology">social-psychology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2259722-p-j-sullivan?shelf=emotions">emotions</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer62231154" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5433159446860485055">Bertrand Russell was the quintessential rational man. In this book he applies rationality to psychology in a systematic examination of human thinking and motivations. Without denying the importance of external social forces, he concerns himself here with only those factors which lie within the power of the individual mind to change.<br/><br/>Discussing the psychological causes of unhappiness, he concludes that preoccupation with self is the chief culprit. The personality should be directed outward. The in</span>
  <span id="freeText5433159446860485055" style="display:none">Bertrand Russell was the quintessential rational man. In this book he applies rationality to psychology in a systematic examination of human thinking and motivations. Without denying the importance of external social forces, he concerns himself here with only those factors which lie within the power of the individual mind to change.<br /><br />Discussing the psychological causes of unhappiness, he concludes that preoccupation with self is the chief culprit. The personality should be directed outward. The introvert, "with the manifold spectacle of the world spread out before him, turns away and gazes only upon the emptiness within. Fundamental happiness depends more than anything else upon what may be called a friendly interest in persons and things." Not unlike Dale Carnegie's advice! Preoccupations with sin and the "sympathy of the herd" are other causes of misery. He advises a quiet life satisfying to instinct. To Russell, personal happiness was the best hope for ending warfare and other social ills.<br /><br />This book is clear, concise, readable, and very quotable.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5433159446860485055" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_114893909" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/114893909" />
    <a title="Matt Riddle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/997210-matt-riddle"><img alt="Matt Riddle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1266128407p2/997210.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/114893909?book_show_action=true">Aug 02, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt Riddle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt Riddle" href="/user/show/997210-matt-riddle">Matt Riddle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer114893909" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16586820073881541380">This book is a bit dated in parts but still packed with goodness.<br/><br/>Some people consider self-love or self-esteem the counter to or cure for self-loathing. Russell makes a very compelling argument that self-esteem and self-loathing are merely flip sides of the same coin of self-absorption. Russell argues that the best and most healthy practice is to think about yourself as little as possible and instead become absorbed by causes and activities outside of yourself. <br/><br/>"Through such interests a man come</span>
  <span id="freeText16586820073881541380" style="display:none">This book is a bit dated in parts but still packed with goodness.<br /><br />Some people consider self-love or self-esteem the counter to or cure for self-loathing. Russell makes a very compelling argument that self-esteem and self-loathing are merely flip sides of the same coin of self-absorption. Russell argues that the best and most healthy practice is to think about yourself as little as possible and instead become absorbed by causes and activities outside of yourself. <br /><br />"Through such interests a man comes to feel himself part of the stream of life, not a hard separate entity like a billiard ball, which can have no relation with other such entities except that of collision."<br /><br />...<br /><br />"Such a man feels himself a citizen of the universe, enjoying freely the spectacle that it offers and the joys that it affords, untroubled by the thought of death because he feels himself not really separate from those who will come after him. It is in such profound instinctive union with the stream of life that the greatest joy is to be found."<br /><br />...<br /><br />"The acts to be recommended from the point of view of the hedonist are on the whole the same as those to be recommended by the sane moralist."<br /><br />Russell makes a very compelling argument that hedonism and rational moralism ultimately direct one to the selfsame lifestyle. It's certainly true that there are many pleasures which do not promote happiness. But those are ultimately much less pleasurable than the pleasures which do. According to Russell, the greatest pleasures in life are afforded by -- in order -- parenthood, family life, work which is pro-social and which one finds interesting, a general appreciation of beauty and goodness in the world around us, and hobbies which are non-destructive and practiced in moderation. In other words, the very pleasures promoted by the moralist.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16586820073881541380" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_265359468" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/265359468" />
    <a title="Snehal Bhagat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/362402-snehal-bhagat"><img alt="Snehal Bhagat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1326107668p2/362402.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/265359468?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Snehal Bhagat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Snehal Bhagat" href="/user/show/362402-snehal-bhagat">Snehal Bhagat</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780871401625" href="/book/show/454750.The_Conquest_of_Happiness">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer265359468" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9525988836613886764">A mathematician-turned-philosopher reflects on what constitutes true happiness, and how to attain it.<br/><br/>Russell is highly regarded in mathematical circles, but I have little idea of what his contribution to philosophy is, or in what light modern psychology views his recipes for achieving a happy life. And recipes they are, arising out of his personal observations. Which probably explains the unappetizing elements of the book- a friend of mine says that if you only ever get to see circles when you a</span>
  <span id="freeText9525988836613886764" style="display:none">A mathematician-turned-philosopher reflects on what constitutes true happiness, and how to attain it.<br /><br />Russell is highly regarded in mathematical circles, but I have little idea of what his contribution to philosophy is, or in what light modern psychology views his recipes for achieving a happy life. And recipes they are, arising out of his personal observations. Which probably explains the unappetizing elements of the book- a friend of mine says that if you only ever get to see circles when you are growing up, when you first see a square, you comprehend it as an imperfect circle. Russell had it kind of rough, growing up.<br /><br />Depending on your stage of life and state of mind, this can be an extremely persuasive book, or a rather trite one. I found it to be a bit of both, but then I don't read a lot of philosophy. Modulo the elements that haven't dated well however, there was sufficient that has resonance for our times. <br /><br />Thus, for Russell, the conquest of happiness is impaired by the "disease of self-absorption.” Indolence won’t bring true happiness, because a certain amount of struggle is essential in life. And just as the Buddha did, he urges us to never be envious of the gifts that others receive, for then one would never have a moment of peace. <br /><br />Conformity sucks, says Russell, and is also an impediment to happiness. And he’d know something about non-conformity, having discovered that his parents lived in a ménage-a-trois when he was very young. <blockquote>A society composed of men and women who do not bow too much to the conventions is a far more interesting society than one in which all behave alike.</blockquote> Take some risks, advises the wise old man, for of all forms of caution, caution in love is perhaps the most fatal to true happiness. Touché.  <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9525988836613886764" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_1702342628" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1702342628" />
    <a title="Tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17817291-tim"><img alt="Tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442015982p2/17817291.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1702342628?book_show_action=true">Jul 18, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim" href="/user/show/17817291-tim">Tim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17817291-tim?shelf=2016">2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17817291-tim?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17817291-tim?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1702342628" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14683495921122524524">Much more the passionate carpe diem approach of Horace than the placid sit down quietly and wait for happiness to alight on you approach of Nathaniel Hawthorne. Even the title is proactive, suggestive of territory being taken in a war with an opposing force.<br/><br/>Russell begins with the notion that happiness begins in something exterior to a person. I would have to agree with him on purely etymological grounds. Happiness is related to the English words 'happenstance' and 'hapless' (both words that ind</span>
  <span id="freeText14683495921122524524" style="display:none">Much more the passionate carpe diem approach of Horace than the placid sit down quietly and wait for happiness to alight on you approach of Nathaniel Hawthorne. Even the title is proactive, suggestive of territory being taken in a war with an opposing force.<br /><br />Russell begins with the notion that happiness begins in something exterior to a person. I would have to agree with him on purely etymological grounds. Happiness is related to the English words 'happenstance' and 'hapless' (both words that indicate things over which you have no control), all of which are derived from the norwegian word 'hap' which means luck or chance. So happiness in its proper sense is very much circumstantial, being grounded in the external environment. You might say happiness is transitory rather than transcendent (rooted in your environment rather than in something beyond it). His aim in writing was to consider ways in which we can maximize our happiness in the place in which we find ourselves by altering our thinking and thus our actions and reactions to the world about us. It is worth noting that he is not considering either ideal or extenuating circumstances but the everyday existence of the average citizen: <br /><br /><i>'In discussing this problem, I shall confine my attention to those who are not subject to any extreme cause of outward misery. I shall assume a sufficient income to secure food and shelter, sufficient health to make ordinary bodily activities possible. I shall not consider the great catastrophes, such as the loss of all one's children, or public disgrace. There are things to be said about such matters, and they are important things, but they belong to a different order from the things I wish to say. My purpose is to suggest a cure for the ordinary day-to-day unhappiness from which most people in civilized countries suffer, and which is all the more unbearable because, having no obvious external cause, it appears largely inescapable.'</i> <br /><br />The book is divided into two halves consisting of a catalogue of things that cause unhappiness and things that cause happiness. Things that cause unhappiness: self-absorption, pessimism, the competitive nature of modern life, boredom, mental, emotional, and physical tiredness, comparing yourself and your lot in life to others, inner conflict, perpetual victim-hood, and peer pressure. Things that cause happiness: curiousity, awe, enthusiasm and appreciation for life, reciprocal affection, parenthood, work, hobbies, and balance. I would mention in passing that at least some of these things are interchangeable. While it is true that things like parenthood and work can be sources of happiness, there are also times when they can be a source of profound unhappiness.<br /><br />There are a few niggling comments I could make. I found his description of Christians to be overly cartoonish. It may be true that some spend all their time obsessively and exhaustively doing a moral inventory of their inner being but it would seem to me a healthy spirituality would consist in a balance of looking outward and looking inward. He also seemed to have it in for introverts in places. The difference between being an introvert or an extrovert is essentially the same as being tall or short, neither condition being superior or inferior to the other but each certainly different from the other. <br /><br />Overall I found this to be a good book. I was actually quite surprised how much of what was written struck a sympathetic nerve. I completely agree with his assessment that it is wrong to consider the pursuit of knowledge only worthwhile if it has some functional purpose behind it (namely making vast quantities of money.) This problem has become even more pronounced since this book was written, perhaps due to increased corporate funding of post secondary educational systems. If you have the time, here is a simple exercise: google the phrase 'Are the humanities.....?' and see what the suggested auto completes are. He was also quite prescient in his analysis of the ability of the media to stigmatize people who have 'the wrong views.' <br /><br />This book might be considered an early example of the self-help genre. A lot of it could be construed as common sense, albeit extremely well articulated common sense. Early in the book Russell takes to task the book of Ecclesiastes (I would contend he entirely misses the point of the book of Ecclesiastes while staring it full in the face) but in light of his views one might consider this a rosier vision of a man 'under the sun.'</span>
  <a data-text-id="14683495921122524524" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText16818895792160610255" style="display:none">The rationalist par excellence Bertrand Russell elaborates on the Conquest of Happiness in a transitioning society, staged on the so-felt struggle between religious morality of the Western world and the growing personal freedom we nowadays often take for granted.     <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_942787005" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/942787005" />
    <a title="Michael P." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2269105-michael-p"><img alt="Michael P." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1278431856p2/2269105.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/942787005?book_show_action=true">May 20, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael P." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael P." href="/user/show/2269105-michael-p">Michael P.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/22209346-the-conquest-of-happiness">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer942787005" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7611853651145015298">Eminent and prolifically published British philosopher Bertrand Russell wrote a self-help book? Yep, and like most self-help books, it has its problems. Russell sensibly approaches the issue of finding happiness as a good philosopher ought, thoroughly and evenhandedly. The books is structured in two parts, first the obstacles to happiness and how to overcome them followed by positive things that people can do to achieve happiness.<br/><br/>The logic is essentially compelling and the advice good, though Ru</span>
  <span id="freeText7611853651145015298" style="display:none">Eminent and prolifically published British philosopher Bertrand Russell wrote a self-help book? Yep, and like most self-help books, it has its problems. Russell sensibly approaches the issue of finding happiness as a good philosopher ought, thoroughly and evenhandedly. The books is structured in two parts, first the obstacles to happiness and how to overcome them followed by positive things that people can do to achieve happiness.<br /><br />The logic is essentially compelling and the advice good, though Russell missed telling readers that every change in their lives will necessitate finding a new balance between the things that make life fulfilling. The book is badly dated. His comments about why tycoons have trouble finding happiness must have been written before the Wall Street crash of 1929, so the book would have seemed odd to his readers in 1930, when it was published. This is an extreme example of how many, even most of Russell's examples seem wrong today. I'm sure these examples had resonance in 1930, but the world has changed so much that most the examples that support (give proof to) his greater points are many of his examples no longer true. This ought to make us skeptical about his greater points, though they resonate with my own struggle for happiness. Russell is essentially right, but so much of the details are now wrong that some readers will not be able to see the proverbial forest past the proverbial trees.<br /><br />There is much sound advice if you can see past this, but ultimately, the book failed to convince me. I have come to a different place on the happiness puzzle. I think that by his standard, Lord Russell would consider me to be happy. I do not consider myself to be. Most days are neither good days nor bad. They are just days, steady, progressing towards goals, fulfilling to a degree, but not especially happy or sad. Some minority of days are very good and some minority are very bad. I no longer consider something as elusive as happiness to be a goal, substituting having a fulfilling day instead because you can't make happiness happen. <br /><br />It would be interesting to have a conversation with Russell to learn if I guess correctly, that this is what he means by happiness, but that is not possible. That word is in the way. My final criticism is that the concept of "happiness" is simply flawed, but that is as much a part of my subjective experience based on the world as I find it as were Russell's dated examples based on the worlds as he found it, so who knows?</span>
  <a data-text-id="7611853651145015298" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_597245742" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/597245742" />
    <a title="Caroline Friedman Levy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13041805-caroline-friedman-levy"><img alt="Caroline Friedman Levy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1348279513p2/13041805.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/597245742?book_show_action=true">Apr 22, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Caroline Friedman Levy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Caroline Friedman Levy" href="/user/show/13041805-caroline-friedman-levy">Caroline Friedman Levy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer597245742" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16599924753030373255">A soothing, Emersonian book filled with Russell's wisdom on "the happy man", pushing one towards impulsivity, friendliness, big-heartedness. To the young writer, "Give up trying to write, and, instead, try not to write. Go out into the world; become a pirate, a king in Borneo, a laborer in Soviet Russia." Quite a bit about the family and what a significant source of happiness Russell's own children were to him, slightly irritating to read while on family vacation and, given the nature of men who</span>
  <span id="freeText16599924753030373255" style="display:none">A soothing, Emersonian book filled with Russell's wisdom on "the happy man", pushing one towards impulsivity, friendliness, big-heartedness. To the young writer, "Give up trying to write, and, instead, try not to write. Go out into the world; become a pirate, a king in Borneo, a laborer in Soviet Russia." Quite a bit about the family and what a significant source of happiness Russell's own children were to him, slightly irritating to read while on family vacation and, given the nature of men who write books on how others should live, was unsurprised to find out he was in fact four times married with one child estranged for several years. Still, he was well ahead of his time and on the right side of history about nearly everything: against World War I, opposed to friends who forgave Bolshevism all its gruesome excesses, turned against pacifism to speak loudly against Hitler, returned to a vocal anti-war stance regarding Vietnam. What I'd most like to absorb from him is his ability to grow old; he died at 97 a healthy 20 years after winning the Nobel prize "in recognition of his varied and significant writings in which he champions humanitarian ideals and freedom of thought." </span>
  <a data-text-id="16599924753030373255" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_638857404" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/638857404" />
    <a title="Momina Masood" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17262906-momina-masood"><img alt="Momina Masood" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1423900868p2/17262906.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/638857404?book_show_action=true">Jun 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Momina Masood" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Momina Masood" href="/user/show/17262906-momina-masood">Momina Masood</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17262906-momina-masood?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer638857404" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14760374787976443721">Finally! This could have been an exceptionally intelligent book if Russell had a little tolerance towards religion. Some of the things he says in this books are perfectly correct, rational, and infallible, but there are other things, as well; that of a lame, idiotic, and unconvincing tendency. The book gets tedious at times, and his harping over 'the man of science' again and again really tested my patience. And another thing was his dreary outlook for the poor, unfortunate 'domesticated wife'! </span>
  <span id="freeText14760374787976443721" style="display:none">Finally! This could have been an exceptionally intelligent book if Russell had a little tolerance towards religion. Some of the things he says in this books are perfectly correct, rational, and infallible, but there are other things, as well; that of a lame, idiotic, and unconvincing tendency. The book gets tedious at times, and his harping over 'the man of science' again and again really tested my patience. And another thing was his dreary outlook for the poor, unfortunate 'domesticated wife'! I could have given this 2.5 stars if he hadn't written all those depressing paragraphs. <br /><br />Despite all of this, I must give him some appreciation for his cool, intelligent writing-style. That was good.<br /><br />But all in all, this is an exhausting book, albeit the few pages. And it isn't worth the effort. <br /><br />Sorry. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13560142972974297051">Such a lovely find this book was. I found this on my father’s bookshelf. The date said bought ‘Nov.24, 99’. It means I was of age 12 when this book was bought. 14 years later now that I am 26, I can truly relate to this book after seeing only a half of life. <br/><br/>At first reading I had no interest in the author, but it was actually after reading a couple of chapters that I wanted to dig into the life of a person who can write such pro-found things about life and lives of people.   <span id="freeText13560142972974297051" style="display:none">Such a lovely find this book was. I found this on my father’s bookshelf. The date said bought ‘Nov.24, 99’. It means I was of age 12 when this book was bought. 14 years later now that I am 26, I can truly relate to this book after seeing only a half of life. <br /><br />At first reading I had no interest in the author, but it was actually after reading a couple of chapters that I wanted to dig into the life of a person who can write such pro-found things about life and lives of people. And after reading few pages about the author I was even more delighted to have come across such a book.<br /><br />It is a lovely read, and not at all boring. Every chapter is filled with thoughts to ponder over and to think that they were written well over 8 decades is just amazing. Mr. Russell truly was a great thinker.<br /><br />I highly recommend this book; it is a great over-haul for both men and women.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('tLMAVM4CK7O8ncb6oCN3asf/RubjWz22C8pLZYWgGMhot6/eC73iHxT1ijTrk/ewLStNZr0TKc1dJyo8LCdyyQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ZHSWBJKoZYCd1DGbgWgxk+6DkP4uUAlkZmPeI19UAR24cDmOVxesLDW8fVXK2LFJBFebfnAYHR8wjr969tNrHA==')})
  }
//]]>

A<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText11505038497657301676\" style=\"display:none\">A leading German metaphysician of the 19th century, Arthur Schopenhauer (1788–1860) exerted an influence far beyond the hermetic world of philosophy, with adherents ranging from Richard Wagner and Friedrich Nietzsche to Leo Tolstoy and Thomas Mann. Among Schopenhauer\'s chief contributions to the field of philosophy are his rejection of the idealism of his contemporaries and his embrace of a practical variety of materialism. He jettisons the traditional philosophic jargon for a brisk, compelling style that employs direct terms to express the metaphysics of the will.In The Wisdom of Life, an essay from Schopenhauer\'s final work, Parerga und Paralipomena (1851), the philosopher favors individual strength of will and independent, reasoned deliberation over the tendency to act on irrational impulses. He examines the ways in which life can be arranged to derive the highest degree of pleasure and success, presents guidelines to achieving this full and rich manner of living, and advises that even a life well lived must always aspire to grander heights. Abounding in subjects of enduring relevance, Schopenhauer\'s highly readable work appears here in an excellent translation.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"11505038497657301676\" href=\"#\" Some of these chapte<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText11715939020337820696\" style=\"display:none\">Between 1974 and 1976, while working on the book To Have Or to Be? at his home in Locarno, Switzerland, the aged Erich Fromm wrote far more manuscript and chapters than were actually used in the book, which was published in 1976. Some of these chapters are contained in the present volume. They deal entirely with the &quot;steps toward being&quot; that the individual can take in order to learn the Art of Being. How can we realize and actualize Love, Reason, and meaningful, productive work? Fromm here offers the Art of Being, a way of living based on authentic self-awareness that comes only through honest self-analysis. Wisely, he warns of the pitfalls of our attaining enlightenment without effort, or believing that life can be lived without pain. The tantalizing &quot;spiritual smorgasbord&quot; offered by our consumer-oriented world, Fromm maintains, only feeds our illusions of &quot;easy awareness.&quot; Confronting the psycho-Gurus who preach these shortcuts to enlightenment, Fromm offers another way to self-awareness and well-being, one based on psychoanalysis and self-awareness through meditation. If the Art of Being - the art of functioning as a whole person - can be considered the supreme goal of life, a breakthrough occurs when we move from narcissistic selfishness and egotism - from having - to psychological and spiritual happiness - being. The Art of Being will be one of the most important works in the Fromm canon for years to come.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"11715939020337820696\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return These were the concerns of Socrates, who spent his days in the ancient Athenian marketplace asking awkward questions, disconcerting the people he met by showing them how little they genuinely understood. This engaging book introduces the great thinkers in Western philosophy and explores their most compelling ideas about the world and how best to live in it.\n\nIn forty brief chapters, Nigel Warburton guides us on a chronological tour of the major ideas in the history of philosophy. He provides interesting and often quirky stories of the lives and deaths of thought-provoking philosophers from Socrates, who chose to die by hemlock poisoning rather than live on without the freedom to think for himself, to Peter Singer, who asks the disquieting philosophical and ethical questions that haunt our own times.\n\nWarburton not only makes philosophy accessible, he offers inspiration to think, argue, reason, and ask in the tradition of Socrates. Leyenda del tiempo es una de las creaciones mayores del teatro de Federico García Lorca. Destaca en ella la contemplación erótico-filosófica del destino humano expresada en el eterno juego del tiempo, el amor y la muerte. <span id="freeTextContainer12120189395240398563">Bertrand Arthur William Russell, 3rd Earl Russell, OM, FRS, was a Welsh philosopher, historian, logician, mathematician, advocate for social reform, pacifist, and prominent rationalist. Although he was usually regarded as English, as he spent the majority of his life in England, he was born in Wales, where he also died.<br/><br/>He was awarded the Nobel Prize in Literature in 1950 "in recognition of his var</span>
  <span id="freeText12120189395240398563" style="display:none">Bertrand Arthur William Russell, 3rd Earl Russell, OM, FRS, was a Welsh philosopher, historian, logician, mathematician, advocate for social reform, pacifist, and prominent rationalist. Although he was usually regarded as English, as he spent the majority of his life in England, he was born in Wales, where he also died.<br /><br />He was awarded the Nobel Prize in Literature in 1950 "in recognition of his varied and significant writings in which he champions humanitarian ideals and freedom of thought."<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12120189395240398563" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
